<div id="review_2275899176" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2275899176" />
    <a title="Teleseparatist" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3526766-teleseparatist"><img alt="Teleseparatist" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528998170p2/3526766.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2275899176?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teleseparatist" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teleseparatist" href="/user/show/3526766-teleseparatist">Teleseparatist</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3526766-teleseparatist?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2275899176" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18011425373951373537">When I first read Lackey's Cute Magical Horseys, Magic Schools for Angstier-than-Thou Kids and also Occasional Off-Page Orgies (which is how I remember this series from my childhood, okay), I ended up reading Arrows of the Queen and a few later volumes quite a few times, but somehow *never* made my way back to one that is perhaps among the best-known parts of the series: The Last Herald-Mage trilogy.<br/><br/>First, I didn't read them because they weren't available in my favourite library. Then, I didn't </span>
  <span id="freeText18011425373951373537" style="display:none">When I first read Lackey's Cute Magical Horseys, Magic Schools for Angstier-than-Thou Kids and also Occasional Off-Page Orgies (which is how I remember this series from my childhood, okay), I ended up reading Arrows of the Queen and a few later volumes quite a few times, but somehow *never* made my way back to one that is perhaps among the best-known parts of the series: The Last Herald-Mage trilogy.<br /><br />First, I didn't read them because they weren't available in my favourite library. Then, I didn't read them because I assumed they had an unhappy ending. I think the queer content (which I knew about) may have been equal parts a draw and a discouragement. I mean, I'd have read them surreptitiously while feeling very guilty. That was basically my jam anyway. But... the suspected unhappy ending was a dealbreaker*.<br /><br />*I don't actually know if my childhood assumption was correct. This is not really a spoiler. <br /><br />So it was on a complete whim that I decided to give them a try now, to see if nostalgia would work for me and if I'd enjoy this earlier volume (plus, sort of, because I'm occasionally completist). And as I started reading, it turned out these books have been read by far more of my friends than goodreads would lead me to believe. <br /><br />But as to the content... I must confess I'd been hoping for more. I mean, the beginning was delicious: it was so angsty as to be both touching (with its very real [if metaphorical] and detailed depiction of teen queer experience) and hilarious (Vanyel's sartorial woes and adventures; Vanyel wearing BLACK because that's the colour of his soul and also he looks good wearing it). Alas, later the angst became a little overwhelming and prolonged. I appreciate that the bodies (and souls) were shown to have limits of endurance, and how important the depiction of grief and depression can be, but I didn't quite connect with the way all these things were written (I should have read it when I was younger). <br /><br />But my biggest complaint - and perhaps this will sound a little shallow - but I was really just hoping for more wish-fulfillment. More power, more magic (which is there, but mostly at the end), more Valdemar, more <i>Companions</i>. These are my magical-soul-healing-horseys books. I wanted my soul to be healed by the horses. And the Companions here are practically in the background. I seem to recall them having a more central place in Arrow books - is my memory failing me?!<br /><br />I'll probably pick up the next volume at some point when I'm done with some more pressing to-be-reads, but not immediately: because of the horsey shortage. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18011425373951373537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_532676413" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/532676413" />
    <a title="K" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6380232-k"><img alt="K" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445206292p2/6380232.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/532676413?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K" href="/user/show/6380232-k">K</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6380232-k?shelf=lit-sci-fi-fantasy">lit-sci-fi-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6380232-k?shelf=sub-secondary-world">sub-secondary-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6380232-k?shelf=rep-lgbtq">rep-lgbtq</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer532676413" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6146414623213786411">I liked Vanyel. Goodreads consensus seems to be that he's a whiny little shit, and I'm not saying that's not true, but I liked him for his being a whiny little shit. Once in a while you're allowed to be.<br/><br/>Vanyel has bad luck with pretty much everything. He doesn't like up to his father's inflexible ideas of masculinity, he's being beaten for being effeminate, and no one understands him. Two out of the three are actual legitimate grievances, and if you think this angst is overblown just wait until </span>
  <span id="freeText6146414623213786411" style="display:none">I liked Vanyel. Goodreads consensus seems to be that he's a whiny little shit, and I'm not saying that's not true, but I liked him for his being a whiny little shit. Once in a while you're allowed to be.<br /><br />Vanyel has bad luck with pretty much everything. He doesn't like up to his father's inflexible ideas of masculinity, he's being beaten for being effeminate, and no one understands him. Two out of the three are actual legitimate grievances, and if you think this angst is overblown just wait until the halfway point.<br /><br />Vanyel is someone who's been abused. You can cut it however you like, and Lackey never actually goes out and says it, but it's clear from his past and it's clear from his behavior. He's reacted in pretty much the only way he knows how: pushing people away. He's kind of a douche in the process, but he's fifteen and while not all fifteen-year-olds are like that, I definitely was so it's kind of hard to hold against him. And, really, I found Vanyel's brooding fits to add some lighthearted amusement to an otherwise overly angsty book.<br /><br />And I do mean angsty; tragedy after tragedy befalls our intrepid hero, and while I really enjoy reading about characters being miserable, it's overblown and so poorly handled that by the time <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Vanyel is trying to kill himself by cutting his wrists the wrong way <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I'm closer to laughter than tears. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Add to the fact that I found Vanyel's relationship with Tylendel to be not only inorganic and hollow but also co-dependent and emotionally manipulative I wasn't too sad to see 'lendel go. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I think part of the problem is that these books aren't particularly well-written. The dialogue is solid, even <i>if</i> Lackey <i>doesn't need</i> to <i>italicize</i> every <i>other</i> word to get her <i>point</i> across (really, this is just bad form; it's juvenile and it interrupts the flow of dialogue). The world is an interesting one when the book bothers to explain it and when that explanation isn't in the form of an info dump (hint: it's not often). The plot would be engaging if it was more refined -- the final confrontation came out of nowhere, there were <i>deus ex machina</i> magic bird people, and it just felt like the author was in such a hurry to get to the tragic, magical parts that she didn't bother to develop the buildup to those scenes. The problem with that being if there's no investment on the reader's part, the emotional payoff falls flat. Add all that into Vanyel, that character who I liked, being unconscious for half the book, and it makes <i>Magic's Pawn</i> a bit of a haphazard mess of a novel, far more like a series of short stories than an actual book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6146414623213786411" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2106354672" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2106354672" />
    <a title="Allison Hurd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14495279-allison-hurd"><img alt="Allison Hurd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530670583p2/14495279.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2106354672?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allison Hurd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allison Hurd" href="/user/show/14495279-allison-hurd">Allison Hurd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6669422-magic-s-pawn">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=fem-author">fem-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=lgbtqia-mc">lgbtqia-mc</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2106354672" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1566155050913690171">An emotionally destructive book. It started out so typically YA and then was like "surprise! Not a YA, this is open heart surgery. BTW we just ran out of anesthesia. Strap in!"<br/><br/><b>CONTENT WARNING</b>: <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ Homophobia, child abuse, suicide, death of a loved one.  <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br/><br/>Things to love:<br/>-<b>Van and Lendel</b>. They are a great young couple who work to bolster each other. It's wonderful to see relationships built on mutual respect and affection, when so often in books it seems there's a skewed d</span>
  <span id="freeText1566155050913690171" style="display:none">An emotionally destructive book. It started out so typically YA and then was like "surprise! Not a YA, this is open heart surgery. BTW we just ran out of anesthesia. Strap in!"<br /><br /><b>CONTENT WARNING</b>: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Homophobia, child abuse, suicide, death of a loved one.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Things to love:<br />-<b>Van and Lendel</b>. They are a great young couple who work to bolster each other. It's wonderful to see relationships built on mutual respect and affection, when so often in books it seems there's a skewed dynamic.<br /><br />-<b>Savil</b>. She's tough and blunt but doing her best. She's wonderfully flawed but the currents of her love run deep.<br /><br />-<b>K'Tevra &amp; Companions</b>. A lot of this is standard fantasy, but the bonding of magic creatures to their mages, and the setting of k'Tevra were lovely. Great elements to distinguish the "castle and tower" type novels.<br /><br />-<b>The story</b>. Usually there's a formula to these stories. This one breaks the formula and dives head long into sadness that usually only occurs once in a hero's story. We're led to believe that the characters are not to be strangers to heartache, and their struggles to remain sane are as tangible a threat as the bad guys casting magic. It's worth noting that Mercedes does an excellent job following us down with her tragedies. If you want to see what non-exploitative trauma looks like, this is a perfect example.<br /><br />Things that made it hard to immerse:<br /><br />-<b>The narrative choices</b>. The long internal monologues in italics, the overuse of em dashes and stress words, and the way Mindspeech was denoted made some of the reading difficult to follow. Also there could have been more clarity as to who was speaking when as there were some complex sentences with very few proper names.<br /><br />-<b>The first 20%</b>. Reads like a super whiny YA for the first few chapters. Keep going. It gets way better.<br /><br />-<b>The pacing</b>. I think we could have slowed a few things down and added a bit more meat. It was devastating as is, and communicated the important things, but the world is rich, and several big reveals were done via exposition after it had happened. I think it would have been much more powerful if we understood beforehand what was happening and why it would be a big deal.<br /><br />Such a terribly sad book, but it was refreshing to have gay protagonists (and more than just one!!), strong women, loving relationships, and an author unafraid to explore human sorrow. I will definitely be continuing the series and recommend it highly, as long as you're okay with a good cry.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1566155050913690171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_623301474" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/623301474" />
    <a title="Juushika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/616321-juushika"><img alt="Juushika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541586729p2/616321.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/623301474?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Juushika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Juushika" href="/user/show/616321-juushika">Juushika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=status-borrowed">status-borrowed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=genre-fantasy">genre-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=trope-companion-animal">trope-companion-animal</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer623301474" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7603192958281679561">Smothered by his oppressive country family, Vanyel is given a second chance when he's sent to the city to live with his aunt, a Herald instructor. But his maturation begets tragedy, and magic, and perhaps war. <i>Magic's Pawn</i> seems intended to be a powerful emotional journey of love and loss, but Lackey expresses almost no understanding of human emotion. Vanyel lives in emotional extremes, his character growth is delineated and repetitive, and most of all his experience and reactions are both so tr</span>
  <span id="freeText7603192958281679561" style="display:none">Smothered by his oppressive country family, Vanyel is given a second chance when he's sent to the city to live with his aunt, a Herald instructor. But his maturation begets tragedy, and magic, and perhaps war. <i>Magic's Pawn</i> seems intended to be a powerful emotional journey of love and loss, but Lackey expresses almost no understanding of human emotion. Vanyel lives in emotional extremes, his character growth is delineated and repetitive, and most of all his experience and reactions are both so tragic that the book reads as pure--and problematic, given that it exploits links between homosexuality and suffering--melodrama. When I read these books as a teenager, the melodrama worked for me--it's an exaggerated analog to the teenage experience, and the gay protagonist made it in equal parts intriguing and sympathetic. Now, it provides a soap-opera readability but is deeply flawed. Although the emotional journey often feels ad hoc, this trilogy has obvious, even heavy-handed, plot direction--and I will continue it, largely to see how my teenage impressions continue to line up with the text. But I don't recommend it, in whole or part.<br /><br />As a side note, I hadn't realized the similarity between Valdemar and Pern. <i>Magic's Pawn</i> underplays the Herald and Companion structure well-established in other books (although the magical Native American-equivalent which is explored instead is, too, problematic), but both the magic systems and the societies are eerily similar, from their excessive capitalized nouns to their magically-enforced relationships. Of the latter, in this book, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[after Vanyel's first sexual experience he immediately becomes roommates with his lover (at his guardian's suggestion), who turns out to be his soul mate <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>--it's almost as creepy as the rape in Pern.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7603192958281679561" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11646901945353647995">I got the strangest urge to reread this trilogy today. It seems like a bad idea. I loved this in highschool but I am sure it is not going to live up................................... <br/><br/>Here is everything that I disjointedly remember about these books:<br/><br/>They are basically a torrid gay love story for teenage girls. Vanyel is the main character and he is very pretty and VERY angsty and NO ONE UNDERSTANDS HIM so he goes to live with his aunt or something. He wants to be a Bard - Bards are like magical </span>
  <span id="freeText11646901945353647995" style="display:none">I got the strangest urge to reread this trilogy today. It seems like a bad idea. I loved this in highschool but I am sure it is not going to live up................................... <br /><br />Here is everything that I disjointedly remember about these books:<br /><br />They are basically a torrid gay love story for teenage girls. Vanyel is the main character and he is very pretty and VERY angsty and NO ONE UNDERSTANDS HIM so he goes to live with his aunt or something. He wants to be a Bard - Bards are like magical musicians who make people SEE the songs in their MINDS. But he doesn't have the gift so he's just like a really good lute player or whatever. Lame.<br /><br />Anyway he meets his TRUE LOVE named like Tiraniel or some shit. In this 'verse if you meet your true love it's weird and magical and you're like, life-bonded (in one of her other books a dude became life bonded to his magical horse friend and oh my god why am I considering reading this again?) So anyway Tiraniel gets MURDERED BY DARK FORCES and Vanyel like feels his death and shit, and it causes him to angst out SO HARD that he suddenly has EVERY SINGLE KIND of magic. So he's not a NORMAL mage he's a HERALD-MAGE. They're SUPER rare because something has been killing them off. SOME DARK FORCE. <br /><br />Anyway Vanyel remains angsty as fuck but he starts making mage friends. They all have like... power stones that they use to FOCUS THEIR ENERGY. And also every mage has a magical horse friend that is I think telepathic? LIKE I SAID. Shit is porn for teenage girls. <br /><br />ANYWAY I remember very little of the actual plot. Vanyel gets kidnapped and raped at some point, but then he meets SOMEONE NEW!!!!!! to love!!!!!!! And he gets angsty about how he can forget about Tiraniel for this STEFAN fool, but then he realizes that STEFAN IS TIRANIEL REBORN!!!!!!!! Which, the age difference between them is NOT great. Meaning, it is great as in fairly large, but it is not, y'know, good. Anyway so they become life-bonded or whatever? I think Van refuses to tell Stefan that he's the reborn Tiraniel because it might ~*~traumatize him~*~. And then I think Vanyel dies, because he sacrifices himself or something... it's hell of dramatic. Maybe he tells Stefan after all because there's definitely some "I had to live without YOU so now you have to live without ME" going on. <br /><br />In the end, Stefan is old as fuck and riding a horse (a regular one) through the woods where he knew Van's spirit would linger, so that he can die and they can be TOGETHER FOREVER. Okay it's SO trashy, but I had legit feelings about those woods and what they represented? It's the same way I feel when I listen to Iron &amp; Wine songs. Like - weirdly nostalgic somehow? Look, I don't know man, this series had an Effect on me back in the day.</span>

<div id="review_90167358" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/90167358" />
    <a title="Charlotte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3294388-charlotte"><img alt="Charlotte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294587557p2/3294388.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/90167358?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlotte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlotte" href="/user/show/3294388-charlotte">Charlotte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer90167358" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6765753976103947422">There's lots of praise and lots of laughing about Mercedes Lackey's books, either of which apply for those of her books I read alike. But still, despite constantly reoccuring elements that would bug me into stopping to read other books altogether, I never can put one of her books down. It's no different with <i>Magic's Pawn</i>, first in the trilogy on Vanyel, a character in the Valdemar universe.<br/><br/>This book has everything. <i>Absolutely too many italics</i> AND BOLD PRINT, angst, more angst, over the top magic</span>
  <span id="freeText6765753976103947422" style="display:none">There's lots of praise and lots of laughing about Mercedes Lackey's books, either of which apply for those of her books I read alike. But still, despite constantly reoccuring elements that would bug me into stopping to read other books altogether, I never can put one of her books down. It's no different with <i>Magic's Pawn</i>, first in the trilogy on Vanyel, a character in the Valdemar universe.<br /><br />This book has everything. <i>Absolutely too many italics</i> AND BOLD PRINT, angst, more angst, over the top magic, a world that doesn't get half as well described as I usually enjoy it, characters that often are a bit too flat and one-sided and can do a few too many things, constant in-your-face-dialogues mentioning the obvious... and yet, oh, and yet. <br />I don't exactly what it is, but like other books by Mercedes Lackey, they draw me in from the first sentence and that's about it. No chance to stop reading. I couldn't help enjoying this book, not when I first read it with 17, nor now, in an age in which I like to claim I'm free of angst and a mature reader who is rather picky with what she reads.<br />For some reason, there's something in these books that, despite all the flaws I could write huge lists of and rant about for paragraphs, that hits home, right where books are supposed to reach you. And while I just cannot give it a solide 5 star rating, because the writing really could be a bit better, I enjoyed myself more than I'd like to admit re-reading this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6765753976103947422" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1406657803" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1406657803" />
    <a title="fleurette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4692637-fleurette"><img alt="fleurette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1357165343p2/4692637.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1406657803?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="fleurette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="fleurette" href="/user/show/4692637-fleurette">fleurette</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4692637-fleurette?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1406657803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13156024943083745824">I have liked this book more than I have expected. I'm not a typical fantasy fan and I have never read anything by Mercedes Lackey.<br/><br/>First of all, the person who wrote the blurb should be banished from writing anything else in his or her life. We have undoubtedly read two different completely books! The blurb says Vanyel is some kind of self-centered prick who was <u>born</u> with a great magical power while in my book he is a very miserable boy who has never known he has magic in him, and who probably wo</span>
  <span id="freeText13156024943083745824" style="display:none">I have liked this book more than I have expected. I'm not a typical fantasy fan and I have never read anything by Mercedes Lackey.<br /><br />First of all, the person who wrote the blurb should be banished from writing anything else in his or her life. We have undoubtedly read two different completely books! The blurb says Vanyel is some kind of self-centered prick who was <u>born</u> with a great magical power while in my book he is a very miserable boy who has never known he has magic in him, and who probably wouldn't know if not for a great accident. Also, his magic though very dangerous is not used to "a spell that unleashes terrifying wyr-hunters on the land". Yes, we must have read two different books.<br /><br />Vanyel is a character that grabs your heart quite quickly. The whole story has it's very dark parts and Vanyel hurts a lot. Still the book in very entertaining. <br /><br />I'm looking forward to read the next book in this series. I hope that Vanyel will find his peace and happiness again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13156024943083745824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('E2G7zpuIk+z+0iZqgthw/Rqsx3nP2FFauvFdS/scsfgpR9IcINZahFn5kgt1LXoizPMgsQFV3z3hjxv6Nl9DYg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2O0G75ZB+/M8CvG3eTzLyjCIpuAlUb7i3DuSDrXH26Liy289LR8ym5shRdaOycEV5tdBKOvcMIWHRdS/eIQpOA==')})
  }

More, the druid elders warn that Rob and Gamelyn are cast as sworn adversaries, locked in timeless and symbolic struggle for the greenwood’s Maiden.Instead, in a theological twist only a stroppy dissident could envision, Rob swears he’ll defend the sacred woodland of the Horned God and Lady Huntress to his last breath—if his god will let him be lover, not rival, to the one fated as his enemy.But in the eyes of Gamelyn’s Church, sodomy is unthinkable... and the old pagan magics are an evil that must be vanquished.\n------\n\n  With a truly original take on the Robin Hood legends, this historical fantasy series sets Robin the outlaw archer as a queer, chaotic-neutral druid; Marion as pagan queen who is sister but not wife, and their consort a Christian--and thusly conflicted--nobleman.\n  \n<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"3717440295621929435\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8685.Mercedes_Lackey">About Mercedes Lackey</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8685.Mercedes_Lackey"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1215643156p3/8685.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1130473533574564582">Mercedes entered this world on June 24, 1950, in Chicago, had a normal childhood and graduated from Purdue University in 1972. During the late 70's she worked as an artist's model and then went into the computer programming field, ending up with American Airlines in Tulsa, Oklahoma. In addition to her fantasy writing, she has written lyrics for and recorded nearly fifty songs for Firebird Arts & M</span>
  <span id="freeText1130473533574564582" style="display:none">Mercedes entered this world on June 24, 1950, in Chicago, had a normal childhood and graduated from Purdue University in 1972. During the late 70's she worked as an artist's model and then went into the computer programming field, ending up with American Airlines in Tulsa, Oklahoma. In addition to her fantasy writing, she has written lyrics for and recorded nearly fifty songs for Firebird Arts &amp; Music, a small recording company specializing in science fiction folk music.<br /><br />"I'm a storyteller; that's what I see as 'my job'. My stories come out of my characters; how those characters would react to the given situation. Maybe that's why I get letters from readers as young as thirteen and as old as sixty-odd. One of the reasons I write song lyrics is because I see songs as a kind of 'story pill' -- they reduce a story to the barest essentials or encapsulate a particular crucial moment in time. I frequently will write a lyric when I am attempting to get to the heart of a crucial scene; I find that when I have done so, the scene has become absolutely clear in my mind, and I can write exactly what I wanted to say. Another reason is because of the kind of novels I am writing: that is, fantasy, set in an other-world semi-medieval atmosphere. Music is very important to medieval peoples; bards are the chief newsbringers. When I write the 'folk music' of these peoples, I am enriching my whole world, whether I actually use the song in the text or not.<br /><br />"I began writing out of boredom; I continue out of addiction. I can't 'not' write, and as a result I have no social life! I began writing fantasy because I love it, but I try to construct my fantasy worlds with all the care of a 'high-tech' science fiction writer. I apply the principle of TANSTAAFL ['There ain't no such thing as free lunch', credited to Robert Heinlein) to magic, for instance; in my worlds, magic is paid for, and the cost to the magician is frequently a high one. I try to keep my world as solid and real as possible; people deal with stubborn pumps, bugs in the porridge, and love-lives that refuse to become untangled, right along with invading armies and evil magicians. And I try to make all of my characters, even the 'evil magicians,' something more than flat stereotypes. Even evil magicians get up in the night and look for cookies, sometimes.<br /><br />"I suppose that in everything I write I try to expound the creed I gave my character Diana Tregarde in <i>Burning Water</i>:<br /><br />"There's no such thing as 'one, true way'; the only answers worth having are the ones you find for yourself; leave the world better than you found it. Love, freedom, and the chance to do some good -- they're the things worth living and dying for, and if you aren't willing to die for the things worth living for, you might as well turn in your membership in the human race." <br /><br />Also writes as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2504886.Misty_Lackey" rel="nofollow noopener">Misty Lackey</a><br /><br /><a href="http://us.macmillan.com/author/mercedeslackey" rel="nofollow noopener">Author's website</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="1130473533574564582" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
